id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Quantum gravity</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-self-published" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>may contain improper references to <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">self-published sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit">improve it</a> by removing references to unreliable <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources">sources</a>, where they are used inappropriately.</span>  <small><i>(June 2011)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Quantum gravity</b> (<b>QG</b>) is a field of <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical physics</a> that seeks to describe <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> according to the principles of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, and where quantum effects cannot be ignored,<sup id="cite_ref-scholarpedia_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scholarpedia-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> such as near compact astrophysical objects where the effects of gravity are strong.\n</p><p>The current understanding of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> is based on <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/General_theory_of_relativity" class="mw-redirect" title="General theory of relativity">general theory of relativity</a>, which is formulated within the framework of <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">classical physics</a>. On the other hand, the other three <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_force" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundamental force">fundamental forces</a> of physics are described within the framework of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanic" class="mw-redirect" title="Quantum mechanic">quantum mechanics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a>, radically different formalisms for describing physical phenomena.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> It is sometimes argued that a quantum mechanical description of gravity is necessary on the grounds that one cannot consistently couple a classical system to a quantum one.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FLoG_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FLoG-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>11&#8211;12</span></sup>\n</p><p>While a quantum theory of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> may be needed to reconcile general relativity with the principles of quantum mechanics, difficulties arise when applying the usual prescriptions of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a> to the force of gravity via <a href="/wiki/Graviton" title="Graviton">graviton</a> bosons.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The problem is that the theory one gets in this way is not <a href="/wiki/Renormalizable" class="mw-redirect" title="Renormalizable">renormalizable</a> and therefore cannot be used to make meaningful physical predictions. As a result, theorists have taken up more radical approaches to the problem of quantum gravity, the most popular approaches being <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Loop_quantum_gravity" title="Loop quantum gravity">loop quantum gravity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Although some quantum gravity theories, such as <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a>, try to unify gravity with the other <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_force" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundamental force">fundamental forces</a>, others, such as <a href="/wiki/Loop_quantum_gravity" title="Loop quantum gravity">loop quantum gravity</a>, make no such attempt; instead, they make an effort to quantize the gravitational field while it is kept separate from the other forces.\n</p><p>Strictly speaking, the aim of quantum gravity is only to describe the quantum behavior of the gravitational field and should not be confused with the objective of <a href="/wiki/Grand_Unified_Theory" title="Grand Unified Theory">unifying all fundamental interactions</a> into a single mathematical framework.  A theory of quantum gravity that is also a <a href="/wiki/Grand_Unified_Theory" title="Grand Unified Theory">grand unification</a> of all known interactions is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_everything" title="Theory of everything">The Theory of Everything</a> (TOE).  While any substantial improvement into the present understanding of gravity would aid further work towards unification, the study of quantum gravity is a field in its own right with various branches having different approaches to unification.\n</p><p>One of the difficulties of formulating a quantum gravity theory is that quantum gravitational effects only appear at length scales near the <a href="/wiki/Planck_scale" class="mw-redirect" title="Planck scale">Planck scale</a>, around 10<sup>\xe2\x88\x9235</sup> meter, a scale far smaller, and equivalently far larger in energy, than those currently accessible by high energy <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a>.  Therefore physicists lack experimental data which could distinguish between the competing theories which have been proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" <span class="toctext">Experimental tests</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table style="float:right; width:30%; margin:0em 1em 1em 1em; background:#eee; border:#ccc solid">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/File:Question_dropshade.png" class="image"><img alt="Question dropshade.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/20px-Question_dropshade.png" width="20" height="20" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/30px-Question_dropshade.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/40px-Question_dropshade.png 2x" data-file-width="50" data-file-height="50" /></a></td>\n<td><b>Unsolved problem in physics</b>:<br /><div style="padding:5px; font-style:italic;">How can the theory of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> be merged with the theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> / <a href="/wiki/Gravitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitation">gravitational</a> force and remain correct at microscopic length scales? What verifiable predictions does any theory of quantum gravity make?</div><small><a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">(more unsolved problems in physics)</a></small>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:482px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Quantum_gravity.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Quantum_gravity.svg/480px-Quantum_gravity.svg.png" width="480" height="314" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Quantum_gravity.svg/720px-Quantum_gravity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Quantum_gravity.svg/960px-Quantum_gravity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1279" data-file-height="836" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Quantum_gravity.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram showing where quantum gravity sits in the hierarchy of physics theories</div></div></div>\n<p>Much of the difficulty in meshing these theories at all energy scales comes from the different assumptions that these theories make on how the universe works. General relativity models gravity as curvature of <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a>: in the slogan of <a href="/wiki/John_Archibald_Wheeler" title="John Archibald Wheeler">John Archibald Wheeler</a>, "Spacetime tells matter how to move; matter tells spacetime how to curve."<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, quantum field theory is typically formulated in the <i>flat</i> spacetime used in <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a>. No theory has yet proven successful in describing the general situation where the dynamics of matter, modeled with quantum mechanics, affect the curvature of spacetime. If one attempts to treat gravity as simply another quantum field, the resulting theory is not <a href="/wiki/Renormalization" title="Renormalization">renormalizable</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Even in the simpler case where the curvature of spacetime is fixed <i>a priori,</i> developing quantum field theory becomes more mathematically challenging, and many ideas physicists use in quantum field theory on flat spacetime are no longer applicable.<sup id="cite_ref-Wald_1994_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wald_1994-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is widely hoped that a theory of quantum gravity would allow us to understand problems of very high energy and very small dimensions of space, such as the behavior of <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">origin of the universe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-scholarpedia_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scholarpedia-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_mechanics_and_general_relativity">Quantum mechanics and general relativity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Quantum mechanics and general relativity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gravity_Probe_B.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Gravity_Probe_B.jpg/150px-Gravity_Probe_B.jpg" width="150" height="291" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Gravity_Probe_B.jpg/225px-Gravity_Probe_B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Gravity_Probe_B.jpg/300px-Gravity_Probe_B.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1228" data-file-height="2385" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gravity_Probe_B.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gravity_Probe_B" title="Gravity Probe B">Gravity Probe B</a> (GP-B) has measured spacetime curvature near Earth to test related models in application of Einstein\'s general theory of relativity.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Graviton">Graviton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Graviton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Graviton" title="Graviton">Graviton</a></div>\n<p>Popularly harmonizing the theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> that describes gravitation, and applications to large-scale structures like <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planets" class="mw-redirect" title="Planets">planets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Galaxies" class="mw-redirect" title="Galaxies">galaxies</a> with <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, that describes the other three <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundamental forces">fundamental forces</a> acting on the <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atomic</a> scale, quantum mechanics and general relativity can seem fundamentally incompatible. Also, demonstrations of the structure of general relativity essentially follows inevitably from the quantum mechanics of interacting <a href="/wiki/Theoretical" class="mw-redirect" title="Theoretical">theoretical</a> spin-2 massless particles called <a href="/wiki/Graviton" title="Graviton">gravitons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kraichnan1955_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kraichnan1955-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gupta1954_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta1954-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gupta1957_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta1957-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gupta1962_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta1962-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Deser1970_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deser1970-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>No concrete proof of gravitons exists, but quantized theories of matter may necessitate their existence.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The observation that all <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundamental forces">fundamental forces</a> except gravity have one or more known <a href="/wiki/Messenger_particles" class="mw-redirect" title="Messenger particles">messenger particles</a> leads researchers to believe that at least one must exist. This hypothetical particle is known as the <i>graviton</i>. The predicted find would result in the classification of the graviton as a <a href="/wiki/Force_particle" class="mw-redirect" title="Force particle">force particle</a> similar to the <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a> of the electromagnetic interaction. Many of the accepted notions of a unified theory of physics since the 1970s assume, and to some degree depend upon, the existence of the graviton. These include <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Superstring_theory" title="Superstring theory">superstring theory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/M-theory" title="M-theory">M-theory</a>. Detection of gravitons would validate these various lines of research to unify quantum mechanics and relativity theory.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Weinberg%E2%80%93Witten_theorem" title="Weinberg\xe2\x80\x93Witten theorem">Weinberg\xe2\x80\x93Witten theorem</a> places some constraints on theories in which <a href="/wiki/Composite_gravity" title="Composite gravity">the graviton is a composite particle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dilaton">Dilaton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Dilaton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dilaton" title="Dilaton">Dilaton</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dilaton" title="Dilaton">dilaton</a> made its first appearance in <a href="/wiki/Kaluza%E2%80%93Klein_theory" title="Kaluza\xe2\x80\x93Klein theory">Kaluza\xe2\x80\x93Klein theory</a>, a five-dimensional theory that combined <a href="/wiki/Gravitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a>. It appears in <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a>. However, it\'s become central to the lower-dimensional many-bodied gravity problem<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> based on the field theoretic approach of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Jackiw" title="Roman Jackiw">Roman Jackiw</a>. The impetus arose from the fact that complete analytical solutions for the metric of a covariant <i>N</i>-body system have proven elusive in general relativity. To simplify the problem, the number of dimensions was lowered to <i>1+1</i> - one spatial dimension and one temporal dimension. This model problem, known as <a href="/wiki/R%3DT_model" class="mw-redirect" title="R=T model"><i>R=T</i> theory</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> as opposed to the general <i>G=T</i> theory, was amenable to exact solutions in terms of a generalization of the <a href="/wiki/Lambert_W_function" title="Lambert W function">Lambert W function</a>.  Also, the field equation governing the dilaton, derived from <a href="/wiki/Differential_geometry" title="Differential geometry">differential geometry</a>, as the <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schr\xc3\xb6dinger equation">Schr\xc3\xb6dinger equation</a> could be amenable to quantization.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This combines gravity, quantization, and even the electromagnetic interaction, promising ingredients of a fundamental physical theory. This outcome revealed a previously unknown and already existing natural link between general relativity and quantum mechanics. There lacks clarity in the generalization of this theory to <i>3+1</i> dimensions. However, a recent derivation in <i>3+1</i> dimensions under the right coordinate conditions  yields a formulation similar to the earlier <i>1+1</i>, a dilaton field governed by the <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Logarithmic Schr\xc3\xb6dinger equation">logarithmic Schr\xc3\xb6dinger equation</a><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> that is seen in <a href="/wiki/Condensed_matter_physics" title="Condensed matter physics">condensed matter physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Superfluidity" title="Superfluidity">superfluids</a>.  The field equations are amenable to such a generalization, as shown with the inclusion of a one-graviton process,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and yield the correct Newtonian limit in <i>d</i> dimensions, but only with a dilaton.  Furthermore, some speculate on the view of the apparent resemblance between the dilaton and the <a href="/wiki/Higgs_boson" title="Higgs boson">Higgs boson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>  However, there needs more experimentation to resolve the relationship between these two particles.  Finally, since this theory can combine gravitational, electromagnetic, and quantum effects, their coupling could potentially lead to a means of testing the theory through cosmology and experimentation.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nonrenormalizability_of_gravity">Nonrenormalizability of gravity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Nonrenormalizability of gravity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Renormalization" title="Renormalization">Renormalization</a></div>\n<p>General relativity, like <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a>, is a <a href="/wiki/Classical_field_theory" title="Classical field theory">classical field theory</a>. One might expect that, as with electromagnetism, the gravitational force should also have a corresponding <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a>.\n</p><p>However, gravity is perturbatively <a href="/wiki/Nonrenormalizable" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonrenormalizable">nonrenormalizable</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FLoG_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FLoG-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>xxxvi&#8211;xxxviii;211&#8211;212</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> For a quantum field theory to be well-defined according to this understanding of the subject, it must be <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_freedom" title="Asymptotic freedom">asymptotically free</a> or <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_safety" class="mw-redirect" title="Asymptotic safety">asymptotically safe</a>. The theory must be characterized by a choice of <i>finitely many</i> parameters, which could, in principle, be set by experiment. For example, in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">quantum electrodynamics</a> these parameters are the charge and mass of the electron, as measured at a particular energy scale.\n</p><p>On the other hand, in quantizing gravity there are, in perturbation theory, <i>infinitely many independent parameters</i> (counterterm coefficients) needed to define the theory. For a given choice of those parameters, one could make sense of the theory, but since it is impossible to conduct infinite experiments to fix the values of every parameter, it has been argued that one does not, in perturbation theory, have a meaningful physical theory. At low energies, the logic of the <a href="/wiki/Renormalization_group" title="Renormalization group">renormalization group</a> tells us that, despite the unknown choices of these infinitely many parameters, quantum gravity will reduce to the usual Einstein theory of general relativity. On the other hand, if we could probe very high energies where quantum effects take over, then <i>every one</i> of the infinitely many unknown parameters would begin to matter, and we could make no predictions at all.\n</p><p>It is conceivable that, in the correct theory of quantum gravity, the infinitely many unknown parameters will reduce to a finite number that can then be measured. One possibility is that normal <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_theory" title="Perturbation theory">perturbation theory</a> is not a reliable guide to the renormalizability of the theory, and that there really <i>is</i> a <a href="/wiki/UV_fixed_point" class="mw-redirect" title="UV fixed point">UV fixed point</a> for gravity. Since this is a question of <a href="/wiki/Non-perturbative" title="Non-perturbative">non-perturbative</a> quantum field theory, it is difficult to find a reliable answer, but some people still pursue this option. Another possibility is that there are new, undiscovered symmetry principles that constrain the parameters and reduce them to a finite set. This is the route taken by <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a>, where all of the excitations of the string essentially manifest themselves as new symmetries.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_gravity_as_an_effective_field_theory">Quantum gravity as an effective field theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Quantum gravity as an effective field theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Effective_field_theory" title="Effective field theory">Effective field theory</a></div>\n<p>In an <a href="/wiki/Effective_field_theory" title="Effective field theory">effective field theory</a>, all but the first few of the infinite set of parameters in a nonrenormalizable theory are suppressed by huge energy scales and hence can be neglected when computing low-energy effects. Thus, at least in the low-energy regime, the model is a predictive quantum field theory.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, many theorists argue that the Standard Model should be regarded as an effective field theory itself, with "nonrenormalizable" interactions suppressed by large energy scales and whose effects have consequently not been observed experimentally.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By treating general relativity as an <a href="/wiki/Effective_field_theory" title="Effective field theory">effective field theory</a>, one can actually make legitimate predictions for quantum gravity, at least for low-energy phenomena. An example is the well-known calculation of the tiny first-order quantum-mechanical correction to the classical Newtonian gravitational potential between two masses.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacetime_background_dependence">Spacetime background dependence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Spacetime background dependence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Background_independence" title="Background independence">Background independence</a></div>\n<p>A fundamental lesson of general relativity is that there is no fixed spacetime background, as found in <a href="/wiki/Newtonian_mechanics" class="mw-redirect" title="Newtonian mechanics">Newtonian mechanics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a>; the spacetime geometry is dynamic. While easy to grasp in principle, this is the hardest idea to understand about general relativity, and its consequences are profound and not fully explored, even at the classical level. To a certain extent, general relativity can be seen to be a <a href="/wiki/Relational_theory" title="Relational theory">relational theory</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> in which the only physically relevant information is the relationship between different events in space-time.\n</p><p>On the other hand, quantum mechanics has depended since its inception on a fixed background (non-dynamic) structure. In the case of quantum mechanics, it is time that is given and not dynamic, just as in Newtonian classical mechanics. In relativistic quantum field theory, just as in classical field theory, <a href="/wiki/Minkowski_spacetime" class="mw-redirect" title="Minkowski spacetime">Minkowski spacetime</a> is the fixed background of the theory.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="String_theory">String theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: String theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Point%26string.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Point%26string.png/220px-Point%26string.png" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Point%26string.png/330px-Point%26string.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Point%26string.png/440px-Point%26string.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Point%26string.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interaction in the subatomic world: <a href="/wiki/World_line" title="World line">world lines</a> of point-like <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">particles</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> or a <a href="/wiki/World_sheet" class="mw-redirect" title="World sheet">world sheet</a> swept up by closed <a href="/wiki/String_(physics)" title="String (physics)">strings</a> in string theory</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">String theory</a> can be seen as a generalization of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a> where instead of point particles, string-like objects propagate in a fixed spacetime background, although the interactions among closed strings give rise to space-time in a dynamical way.\nAlthough string theory had its origins in the study of <a href="/wiki/Quark_confinement" class="mw-redirect" title="Quark confinement">quark confinement</a> and not of quantum gravity, it was soon discovered that the string spectrum contains the <a href="/wiki/Graviton" title="Graviton">graviton</a>, and that "condensation" of certain vibration modes of strings is equivalent to a modification of the original background. In this sense, string perturbation theory exhibits exactly the features one would expect of a <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_theory" title="Perturbation theory">perturbation theory</a> that may exhibit a strong dependence on asymptotics (as seen, for example, in the <a href="/wiki/AdS/CFT" class="mw-redirect" title="AdS/CFT">AdS/CFT</a> correspondence) which is a weak form of <a href="/wiki/Background_independence" title="Background independence">background dependence</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Background_independent_theories">Background independent theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Background independent theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Loop_quantum_gravity" title="Loop quantum gravity">Loop quantum gravity</a> is the fruit of an effort to formulate a <a href="/wiki/Background-independent" class="mw-redirect" title="Background-independent">background-independent</a> quantum theory.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Topological_quantum_field_theory" title="Topological quantum field theory">Topological quantum field theory</a> provided an example of background-independent quantum theory, but with no local degrees of freedom, and only finitely many degrees of freedom globally. This is inadequate to describe gravity in 3+1 dimensions, which has local degrees of freedom according to general relativity. In 2+1 dimensions, however, gravity is a topological field theory, and it has been successfully quantized in several different ways, including <a href="/wiki/Spin_network" title="Spin network">spin networks</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Semi-classical_quantum_gravity">Semi-classical quantum gravity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Semi-classical quantum gravity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">Quantum field theory</a> on curved (non-Minkowskian) backgrounds, while not a full quantum theory of gravity, has shown many promising early results. In an analogous way to the development of quantum electrodynamics in the early part of the 20th century (when physicists considered quantum mechanics in classical electromagnetic fields), the consideration of quantum field theory on a curved background has led to predictions such as black hole radiation.\n</p><p>Phenomena such as the <a href="/wiki/Unruh_effect" title="Unruh effect">Unruh effect</a>, in which particles exist in certain accelerating frames but not in stationary ones, do not pose any difficulty when considered on a curved background (the Unruh effect occurs even in flat Minkowskian backgrounds). The vacuum state is the state with the least energy (and may or may not contain particles).\nSee <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory_in_curved_spacetime" title="Quantum field theory in curved spacetime">Quantum field theory in curved spacetime</a> for a more complete discussion.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Problem_of_Time">Problem of Time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Problem of Time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_Time" class="mw-redirect" title="Problem of Time">Problem of Time</a></div><p>A conceptual difficulty in combining quantum mechanics with general relativity arises from the contrasting role of time within these two frameworks. In quantum theories time acts as an independent background through which states evolve, with the <a href="/wiki/Hamiltonian_(quantum_mechanics)" title="Hamiltonian (quantum mechanics)">Hamiltonian operator</a> acting as the <a href="/wiki/Translation_operator_(quantum_mechanics)" title="Translation operator (quantum mechanics)">generator of infinitesimal translations</a> of quantum states through time.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, general relativity <a href="/wiki/Einstein_field_equations" title="Einstein field equations">treats time as a dynamical variable</a> which interacts directly with matter and moreover requires the Hamiltonian constraint to vanish,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> removing any possibility of employing a notion of time similar to that in quantum theory.\n</p><h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Candidate_theories">Candidate theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Candidate theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>There are a number of proposed quantum gravity theories.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Currently, there is still no complete and consistent quantum theory of gravity, and the candidate models still need to overcome major formal and conceptual problems. They also face the common problem that, as yet, there is no way to put quantum gravity predictions to experimental tests, although there is hope for this to change as future data from cosmological observations and particle physics experiments becomes available.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="String_theory_2">String theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: String theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">String theory</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Calabi-Yau.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Calabi-Yau.png/220px-Calabi-Yau.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Calabi-Yau.png/330px-Calabi-Yau.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Calabi-Yau.png/440px-Calabi-Yau.png 2x" data-file-width="840" data-file-height="840" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Calabi-Yau.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Projection of a <a href="/wiki/Calabi%E2%80%93Yau_manifold" title="Calabi\xe2\x80\x93Yau manifold">Calabi\xe2\x80\x93Yau manifold</a>, one of the ways of <a href="/wiki/Compactification_(physics)" title="Compactification (physics)">compactifying</a> the extra dimensions posited by string theory</div></div></div>\n<p>One suggested starting point is ordinary quantum field theories which are successful in describing the other three basic fundamental forces in the context of the <a href="/wiki/Standard_model" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard model">standard model</a> of <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle_physics" class="mw-redirect" title="Elementary particle physics">elementary particle physics</a>. However, while this leads to an acceptable <a href="/wiki/Effective_field_theory" title="Effective field theory">effective (quantum) field theory</a> of gravity at low energies,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> gravity turns out to be much more problematic at higher energies. For ordinary field theories such as <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">quantum electrodynamics</a>, a technique known as <a href="/wiki/Renormalization" title="Renormalization">renormalization</a> is an integral part of deriving predictions which take into account higher-energy contributions,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> but gravity turns out to be <a href="/wiki/Nonrenormalizable" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonrenormalizable">nonrenormalizable</a>: at high energies, applying the recipes of ordinary quantum field theory yields models that are devoid of all predictive power.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One attempt to overcome these limitations is to replace ordinary <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a>, which is based on the classical concept of a <a href="/wiki/Point_particle" title="Point particle">point particle</a>, with a quantum theory of one-dimensional extended objects: <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> At the energies reached in current experiments, these strings are indistinguishable from point-like particles, but, crucially, different <a href="/wiki/Normal_mode" title="Normal mode">modes</a> of oscillation of one and the same type of fundamental string appear as particles with different (<a href="/wiki/Electric" class="mw-redirect" title="Electric">electric</a> and other) <a href="/wiki/Charge_(physics)" title="Charge (physics)">charges</a>. In this way, string theory promises to be a <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_everything" title="Theory of everything">unified description</a> of all particles and interactions.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The theory is successful in that one mode will always correspond to a <a href="/wiki/Graviton" title="Graviton">graviton</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Messenger_particle" class="mw-redirect" title="Messenger particle">messenger particle</a> of gravity; however, the price of this success are unusual features such as six extra dimensions of space in addition to the usual three for space and one for time.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In what is called the <a href="/wiki/History_of_string_theory#1984\xe2\x80\x931989:_first_superstring_revolution" title="History of string theory">second superstring revolution</a>, it was conjectured that both string theory and a unification of general relativity and <a href="/wiki/Supersymmetry" title="Supersymmetry">supersymmetry</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Supergravity" title="Supergravity">supergravity</a><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> form part of a hypothesized eleven-dimensional model known as <a href="/wiki/M-theory" title="M-theory">M-theory</a>, which would constitute a uniquely defined and consistent theory of quantum gravity.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> As presently understood, however, string theory admits a very large number (10<sup>500</sup> by some estimates) of consistent vacua, comprising the so-called "<a href="/wiki/String_landscape" class="mw-redirect" title="String landscape">string landscape</a>". Sorting through this large family of solutions remains a major challenge.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loop_quantum_gravity">Loop quantum gravity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Loop quantum gravity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Loop_quantum_gravity" title="Loop quantum gravity">Loop quantum gravity</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spin_network.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Spin_network.svg/200px-Spin_network.svg.png" width="200" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Spin_network.svg/300px-Spin_network.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Spin_network.svg/400px-Spin_network.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="702" data-file-height="964" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spin_network.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Simple <a href="/wiki/Spin_network" title="Spin network">spin network</a> of the type used in loop quantum gravity</div></div></div>\n<p>Loop quantum gravity seriously considers general relativity\'s insight that spacetime is a dynamical field and is therefore a quantum object.  Its second idea is that the quantum discreteness that determines the particle-like behavior of other field theories (for instance, the photons of the electromagnetic field) also affects the structure of space.\n</p><p>The main result of loop quantum gravity is the derivation of a granular structure of space at the Planck length.  This is derived from following considerations: In the case of electromagnetism, the quantum operator representing the energy of each frequency of the field has a discrete spectrum. Thus the energy of each frequency is quantized, and the quanta are the photons. In the case of gravity, the operators representing the area and the volume of each surface or space region likewise have discrete spectrum. Thus area and volume of any portion of space are also quantized, where the quanta are elementary quanta of space.  It follows, then, that spacetime has an elementary quantum granular structure at the Planck scale, which cuts off the ultraviolet infinities of quantum field theory.\n</p><p>The quantum state of spacetime is described in the theory by means of a mathematical structure called <a href="/wiki/Spin_network" title="Spin network">spin networks</a>. Spin networks were initially introduced by <a href="/wiki/Roger_Penrose" title="Roger Penrose">Roger Penrose</a> in abstract form, and later shown by <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Rovelli" title="Carlo Rovelli">Carlo Rovelli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lee_Smolin" title="Lee Smolin">Lee Smolin</a> to derive naturally from a non-perturbative quantization of general relativity.  Spin networks do not represent quantum states of a field in spacetime: they represent directly quantum states of spacetime.\n</p><p>The theory is based on the reformulation of general relativity known as <a href="/wiki/Ashtekar_variables" title="Ashtekar variables">Ashtekar variables</a>, which represent geometric gravity using mathematical analogues of <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\nIn the quantum theory, space is represented by a network structure called a <a href="/wiki/Spin_network" title="Spin network">spin network</a>, evolving over time in discrete steps.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The dynamics of the theory is today constructed in several versions. One version starts with the <a href="/wiki/Canonical_quantization" title="Canonical quantization">canonical quantization</a> of general relativity.  The  analogue of the <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schr\xc3\xb6dinger equation">Schr\xc3\xb6dinger equation</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Wheeler%E2%80%93DeWitt_equation" title="Wheeler\xe2\x80\x93DeWitt equation">Wheeler\xe2\x80\x93DeWitt equation</a>, which can be defined within the theory.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\nIn the covariant, or <a href="/wiki/Spinfoam" class="mw-redirect" title="Spinfoam">spinfoam</a> formulation of the theory, the quantum dynamics is obtained via a sum over discrete versions of spacetime, called spinfoams. These represent histories of spin networks.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_approaches">Other approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Other approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>There are a number of other approaches to quantum gravity. The approaches differ depending on which features of general relativity and quantum theory are accepted unchanged, and which features are modified.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Examples include:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_safety_in_quantum_gravity" title="Asymptotic safety in quantum gravity">Asymptotic safety in quantum gravity</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Euclidean_quantum_gravity" title="Euclidean quantum gravity">Euclidean quantum gravity</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Causal_dynamical_triangulation" title="Causal dynamical triangulation">Causal dynamical triangulation</a><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Causal_fermion_system" title="Causal fermion system">Causal fermion systems</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Causal_sets" title="Causal sets">Causal Set Theory</a></li>\n<li>Covariant Feynman <a href="/wiki/Path_integral_formulation" title="Path integral formulation">path integral</a> approach</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Group_field_theory" title="Group field theory">Group field theory</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Wheeler%E2%80%93DeWitt_equation" title="Wheeler\xe2\x80\x93DeWitt equation">Wheeler\xe2\x80\x93DeWitt equation</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Geometrodynamics" title="Geometrodynamics">Geometrodynamics</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ho%C5%99ava%E2%80%93Lifshitz_gravity" title="Ho\xc5\x99ava\xe2\x80\x93Lifshitz gravity">Ho\xc5\x99ava\xe2\x80\x93Lifshitz gravity</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/MacDowell%E2%80%93Mansouri_action" title="MacDowell\xe2\x80\x93Mansouri action">MacDowell\xe2\x80\x93Mansouri action</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Noncommutative_geometry" title="Noncommutative geometry">Noncommutative geometry</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Path_integral_formulation" title="Path integral formulation">Path-integral</a> based models of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_cosmology" title="Quantum cosmology">quantum cosmology</a><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Regge_calculus" title="Regge calculus">Regge calculus</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Scale_relativity" title="Scale relativity">Scale relativity</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Shape_dynamics" title="Shape dynamics">Shape Dynamics</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/String-net" class="mw-redirect" title="String-net">String-nets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_graphity" class="mw-redirect" title="Quantum graphity">quantum graphity</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Superfluid_vacuum_theory" title="Superfluid vacuum theory">Superfluid vacuum theory</a> a.k.a. theory of <a href="/wiki/BEC_vacuum" class="mw-redirect" title="BEC vacuum">BEC vacuum</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Supergravity" title="Supergravity">Supergravity</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Twistor_theory" title="Twistor theory">Twistor theory</a><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Canonical_quantum_gravity" title="Canonical quantum gravity">Canonical quantum gravity</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/E8_Theory" class="mw-redirect" title="E8 Theory">E8 Theory</a></li>\n<li>Quantum <a href="/wiki/Holonomy" title="Holonomy">holonomy</a> theory<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Experimental_tests">Experimental tests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Experimental tests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>As was emphasized above, quantum gravitational effects are extremely weak and therefore difficult to test. For this reason, the possibility of experimentally testing quantum gravity had not received much attention prior to the late 1990s. However, in the past decade, physicists have realized that evidence for quantum gravitational effects can guide the development of the theory. Since theoretical development has been slow, the field of <a href="/wiki/Phenomenological_quantum_gravity" title="Phenomenological quantum gravity">phenomenological quantum gravity</a>, which studies the possibility of experimental tests, has obtained increased attention.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most widely pursued possibilities for quantum gravity phenomenology include violations of <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_covariance" title="Lorentz covariance">Lorentz invariance</a>, imprints of quantum gravitational effects in the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> (in particular its polarization), and decoherence induced by fluctuations in the <a href="/wiki/Space-time_foam" class="mw-redirect" title="Space-time foam">space-time foam</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/BICEP_and_Keck_Array" title="BICEP and Keck Array">BICEP2 experiment</a> detected what was initially thought to be primordial <a href="/wiki/B-modes" class="mw-redirect" title="B-modes">B-mode polarization</a> caused by <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">gravitational waves</a> in the early universe. Had the signal in fact been primordial in origin, it could have been an indication of quantum gravitational effects, but it soon transpired that the polarization was due to <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">interstellar dust</a> interference.<sup id="cite_ref-nature-20150130_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nature-20150130-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Quantum_gravity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17"
